      B ENIGN OR N OT-B ENIGN OVERFITTING
002   IN T OKEN S ELECTION OF ATTENTION M ECHANISM
003
004
005    Anonymous authors
006    Paper under double-blind review
007
008
009                                                A BSTRACT
010
011            Modern over-parameterized neural networks can be trained to fit the training data
012            perfectly while still maintaining a high generalization performance. This вҖңbenign
013            overfittingвҖқ phenomenon has been studied in a surge of recent theoretical work;
014            however, most of these studies have been limited to linear models or two-layer
015            neural networks. In this work, we analyze benign overfitting in the token selection
016            mechanism of the attention architecture, which characterizes the success of trans-
017            former models. We first show the existence of a benign overfitting solution and
018
               explain its mechanism in the attention architecture. Next, we discuss whether the
               model converges to such a solution, raising the difficulties specific to the attention
019
               architecture. We then present benign overfitting cases and not-benign overfitting
020
               cases by conditioning different scenarios based on the behavior of attention prob-
021            abilities during training. To the best of our knowledge, this is the first study to
022            characterize benign overfitting for the attention mechanism.
023
024
025   1   I NTRODUCTION
026
027   Modern over-parameterized neural networks achieve a high generalization accuracy while perfectly
028   fitting to the training data (Zhang et al., 2021). This вҖңbenign overfittingвҖқ phenomenon has attracted
029   attention over the past few years because it contrasts with the conventional wisdom that achieving
030   better generalization requires balancing training error and model complexity through appropriate
031   regularization techniques, which prevents overfitting to training noise. The theoretical understanding
032
      of benign overfitting is crucial for obtaining insights into over-parameterized networks.
033   There are lines of studies analyzing the benign overfitting phenomenon in various settings, linear
034   regression (Bartlett et al., 2020; Tsigler & Bartlett, 2023), linear classification (Chatterji & Long,
035   2021; Cao et al., 2021), and two-layer neural networks (Frei et al., 2022; Cao et al., 2022). However,
036   the analysis of benign overfitting is limited to these types of architecture. As far as we know, there
037   have been no theoretical analyses on attention architecture, which is a core component of the trans-
038
      former model (Vaswani et al., 2017). Given the current success of transformer models across a wide
      range of fields (Brown et al., 2020; Baevski et al., 2020; Dosovitskiy et al., 2021; Wei et al., 2022b;a;
039
      Touvron et al., 2023; Chowdhery et al., 2023), analyzing the attention mechanism that distinguishes
040
      them from other architectures has become increasingly significant.
041
042   The concept of benign overfitting in transformers is not yet well-defined. We do not even know
043   what aspects should be analyzed in the first place. As a first step towards addressing this problem,
044
      we focus on analyzing token selection, a key property of the attention mechanism that characterizes
045
      transformers. We consider a one-layer attention network f (X) = ОҪ вҠӨ XвҠӨ S(XWвҠӨ p) вҲҲ R, where
      S(В·) is the softmax function, X = (x1 , . . . , xT )вҠӨ вҲҲ RT Г—d is the sequence of input tokens, p вҲҲ Rd
046
      is a tunable token, W вҲҲ RdГ—d is the key-query matrix, and ОҪ вҲҲ Rd is a well-pretrained linear
047
      head, on a separated binary classification task. Here, p corresponds to the [CLS] token or prompt
048   tuning in the application of transformers, and we followed the common setup studied in other topics,
049   such as implicit bias (Tarzanagh et al., 2023a;b; Oymak et al., 2023). Our main contributions are
050   summarized as follows:
051
052   вҖў Existence of Benign Overfitting Solution (Thm 4.1): We first prove the existence of a benign
053     overfitting solution and explain its mechanism in the token selection of attention architecture.
        Specifically, in addition to learning the signal with class information, benign overfitting is achieved

      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

                                                                                             (i)
074   Figure 1: Projection of one selected token per sequence. Each point indicates xt selected by
                                            (i)       (i)
075   attention from each input X(i) = (x1 , . . . , xT )вҠӨ in the direction of class signals Вө+1 and ВөвҲ’1 for
076   the three scenarios of benign overfitting, harmful overfitting, and not overfitting. Top: Training data
077   with label noise. Bottom: Test data. The decision boundary is common because the output head ОҪ is
078   fixed, but the model can select an appropriate token that belongs to the desired output region.
079
080
        by memorizing tokens that can fit the label noise. In this scenario, tokens that are useful for
081     class prediction are selected for the unseen data, but for noisy data in the training set, tokens
082     that can adapt to the noise are picked, suppressing the class-relevant tokens. This aligns with the
083     conventional understanding of benign overfitting: the principal components explain the overall
084     prediction, and noise memorization accounts for fitting noise. Please refer to Figure 1 for an
085     explanation of benign overfitting in token selection, as well as other scenarios.
086   вҖў Convergence with Gradient Descent (Thm 4.2): We discuss whether the model weights can
087     converge to such a benign overfitting solution with gradient descent. Two factors specific to at-
088     tention models complicate the straightforward application of existing analysis: (i) the existence of
089     local minima, which trap the optimization process, and (ii) the diminishing updates of desirable
090     parameters as learning progresses and token selection advances. Particularly due to property (ii),
091     the signal updates in each step are not determined based solely on the label noise ratio but rather
092     depend on the other tokens and training data in a complex manner. In this work, we show that
093
        the one-layer attention can be trained to fit the training data with gradient descent. Then, by con-
        ditioning different scenarios of the training trajectory, we present cases where benign overfitting
094
        occurs and cases where it leads to harmful overfitting.
095
096   From a practical perspective, benign overfitting solely through the token selection mechanism has
097   implications, particularly in parameter-efficient fine-tuning. For example, prompt-tuning (Li &
098   Liang, 2021; Lester et al., 2021) trains only the tunable input tokens, and LoRA (Hu et al., 2022)
099   focuses on training only the attention weights. The presence of benign overfitting suggests that
100   adapting the model to low-quality downstream tasks with label noise would not be problematic in
101   terms of generalization. In this paper, we consider binary classification for simplicity of discus-
102
      sion, but as shown in Section F, it can be extended to the multi-class setting without fundamentally
      modifying the argument.
103
104
105   2   R ELATED W ORK
106
107   Benign Overfitting. The success of modern over-parameterized models has led to numerous stud-
      ies attempting to understand why and when benign overfitting occurs. The analysis is interesting


                                                        2
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


108
      because the standard generalization bound based on uniform convergence does not explain this phe-
109   nomenon well (Nagarajan & Kolter, 2019). For comprehensive surveys of the literature on this
110   topic, please see work such as Bartlett et al. (2021); Belkin (2021). Please also refer to Table 2 in
111   the appendix for the comparison of existing work, including ours.
112
      Benign overfitting in regression has been studied in the linear model (Bartlett et al., 2020; Hastie
113
      et al., 2022) and kernel regression (Liang & Rakhlin, 2020; Tsigler & Bartlett, 2023). It is com-
114
      plicated to analyze the classification task because the explicit formula for the min-norm separator
115   is not obtained. One approach is to track the training dynamics with gradient descent in the linear
116   classifier and two-layer neural network (Chatterji & Long, 2021; Frei et al., 2022; Xu & Gu, 2023;
117   Cao et al., 2022; Kou et al., 2023; George et al., 2023; Meng et al., 2024; Xu et al., 2024). Another
118   line of work is built on the results of implicit bias to the max-margin solution (Cao et al., 2021; Wang
119   et al., 2021; Wang & Thrampoulidis, 2022; Frei et al., 2023a). These studies base their discussion
120   of convergence on existing research on implicit bias (Soudry et al., 2018; Ji & Telgarsky, 2019; Lyu
121   & Li, 2020; Frei et al., 2023b). Specifically, they analyze the properties of the solution through the
122   KKT conditions of the max-margin problem in order to examine whether the solution at convergence
123
      shows benign overfitting or not. For instance, in the setting of support vector machine, Hsu et al.
      (2021); Muthukumar et al. (2021) have shown that all training points become support vectors in a
124
      high-dimensional setup. The analysis becomes more challenging in the case of general deep neural
125
      networks, and Zhu et al. (2023) conducts an analysis under the neural tangent kernel (NTK) regime.
126
127   Emerging research directions have explored the benignity of overfitting, given that real-world mod-
128   els do not always exhibit benign overfitting. Mallinar et al. (2022) introduced the term вҖңtempered
129
      overfittingвҖқ to analyze the overfitting between benign and catastrophic. Wen et al. (2023) examined
      tempered overfitting for the mild-overparameterized model, and Kornowski et al. (2024) investigated
130
      it for a two-layer ReLU neural network with low input dimensionality.
131
132
      Token-selection in Attention. The attention mechanism is a core architecture in the transformer
133
      model, and theoretical analyses from the perspectives of generalization ability (Jelassi et al., 2022; Li
134
      et al., 2023a;b) and expressive ability (Yun et al., 2020a;b; Dong et al., 2021) have been progressing
135   in recent years. The most related line of work to ours deals with the implicit bias of gradient descent
136   in training a one-layer attention model (Tarzanagh et al., 2023a;b; Li et al., 2024; Vasudeva et al.,
137   2024). While these studies share common aspects with our work, their primary focus is optimizing
138   the given training set without considering the underlying distribution (see Section 4.3 for details).
139   Furthermore, our work is also influenced by the problem setting of (Oymak et al., 2023). However,
140   they discuss the importance of the softmax function in the attention mechanism, whereas our work
141   is largely different in that we focus on the fitting to label noise and benign overfitting.
142

183   3.3   DATA M ODEL
184
185   In the analysis of benign overfitting, we typically need to consider the specific shape of the un-
186
      derlying data distribution to evaluate the generalization error without using a uniform convergence
      argument. Data models based on signal and noise widely appear in the existing benign overfitting
187
      studies (Chatterji & Long, 2021; Frei et al., 2022; Cao et al., 2022), and ours is a natural extension
188
      to the sequential inputs of the attention model. Such data models based on signal and noise are not
189   limited to the analysis of benign overfitting but are also commonly observed in other analyses of
190   attention architecture (Jelassi et al., 2022; Li et al., 2023a; Oymak et al., 2023).
191
192
      We consider the following data distribution P defined over (X, Y ) вҲҲ RT Г—d Г— {Вұ1}. In this paper,
      we consider binary classification for simplicity of discussion, but the same argument applies to the
193
      multi-class case. Please refer to Section F in the appendix for more details.
194
195   Definition 3.1. Let Вө+1 , ВөвҲ’1 вҲҲ Rd be fixed signal vectors representing the class of each data point.
196   The input X = [x1 , . . . , xT ]вҠӨ вҲҲ RT Г—d has T tokens that are split into three groups: relevant token
197   R вҲҲ [T ] containing the strong signal for true class, weakly relevant token W вҲҲ [T ] \ R containing
198
      weak class signals, and irrelevant token I = [T ] \ (R вҲӘ W) containing only noise. Let clean
      distribution P вҲ— be the distribution over RT Г—d Г— {Вұ1} such that (X, Y вҲ— ) is sampled as follows:
199
200         1. The clean label Y вҲ— is sampled from a uniform distribution on {Вұ1}.
201
202         2. The noise vectors (Пөt )tвҲҲ[T ] are sampled independently from N (0, ОЈ) for some diagonal
203            matrix ОЈ вҲҲ RdГ—d . We assume the diagonal elements are positive and of constant order.
204
            3. The relevant tokens xt , t вҲҲ R are generated with xt = ВөY вҲ— + Пөt .
205
206         4. The weakly relevant tokens xu , u вҲҲ W are generated with xu = ПҒВөwu + Пөu , where
207            ПҒ = в„Ұ(1) > 0 is a small scale parameter, and (wu )uвҲҲW are sampled from a uniform
208            distribution on {Вұ1}. For simplicity, we assume at least one token xu aligns with each
209            class, which holds with high probability (see Lemma B.9 for details).
210
            5. The irrelevant tokens xv , v вҲҲ I are generated with xv = Пөv .
211
212   Here, we assume the ratios of relevant and weakly relevant tokens are constant and denote them
213   by О¶R = |R|/T вҲҲ [1/T, 1 вҲ’ 1/T ], and О¶W = |W|/T вҲҲ [1/T, 1 вҲ’ 1/T ], respectively. The data
214   distribution P is defined as the label-corrupted version of P вҲ— with the level of label noise О· > 0.
215   The data point (X, Y ) from P is generated by first sampling (X, Y вҲ— ) from clean distribution P вҲ—
      and then setting Y = вҲ’Y вҲ— with probability О· and Y = Y вҲ— with probability 1 вҲ’ О·.

267   3.5   A SSUMPTION ON PARAMETERS
268
269   In this section, we first discuss the necessity of the assumptions on parameters and compare them
      with existing studies, followed by a list of the assumptions used in our work. In analyzing benign


                                                         5
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


270
      overfitting, the balance between memorization and generalization is significant. Therefore, we im-
271   pose assumptions on the balance between the dimensionality Tr(ОЈ) and the signal strength вҲҘВөвҲҘ2 .
272
273   The over-parameterization assumptions (A1) and (AвҖҷ1) are necessary for fitting label noise, and
274
      similar terms nвҲҘВөвҲҘ22 can be found in (Chatterji & Long, 2021; Frei et al., 2022; Kou et al., 2023).
275   Assumptions (A2) and (AвҖҷ2) are required for generalization, while Tr(ОЈ)1/4 in the right-hand side
276   also appears in (Xu & Gu, 2023), and n1/4 Tr(ОЈ)1/4 is found in (Xu et al., 2024). For these as-
277
      sumptions on Tr(ОЈ) and вҲҘВөвҲҘ2 , since this is the first analysis of benign overfitting in the attention
      architecture, the dependence on sequence length T is newly introduced, and the difficulties unique
278
      to this setting, which will be discussed later in Section 4.3, involve stronger assumptions. Assump-
279
      tions (A3) and (AвҖҷ3) are also necessary for fitting label noise, and (AвҖҷ3) is stronger for simplify-
280   ing the conditions in Theorem 4.2. This assumption is non-vacuous because the value of ПҒ can
281   take in a wide range when Tr(ОЈ) and вҲҘВөвҲҘ2 are significantly larger than T and n. For example,
282   n = в„Ұ(polylog(Tr(ОЈ)) is assumed in (Cao et al., 2022), which is based on a similar proof tech-
283   nique to ours. Assumptions (A5) and (A6) are placed to evaluate the class balance in the training
284   data and the amount of noisy data, but in this paper, they are absorbed into the conditions of Eqs.9-13
285   and are not directly used.
286   Now, we state our assumptions in the following. Given a small failure probability Оҙ > 0 and a large
287   enough universal constant C, we make the assumptions for each parameter as follows:

309   4     M AIN R ESULTS
310
311   In this section, we first demonstrate the existence of a benign overfitting solution in Section 4.1
312   and then discuss the convergence of gradient descent towards this benign overfitting solution in
313   Section 4.2. Finally, in Section 4.3, we explain the unique difficulties posed by the attention ar-
314   chitectures in analyzing benign overfitting and provide justification for the conditions used in the
315   theorem and approach adopted in this work.
316
317   4.1    E XISTENCE OF B ENIGN OVERFITTING
318
319   We first show the existence of the solution that exhibits benign overfitting. This result is useful for
320   explaining the key idea that both signal learning and noise memorization are necessary for benign
321
      overfitting in token selection. Here, note that the model simply learning the signal vectors does not
      overfit to noisy data.
322
323   Theorem 4.1 (Existence of benign overfitting solution). Suppose that the assumptions (A1)-(A6)
      hold. Let the linear head ОҪ вҲқ Вө+1 вҲ’ВөвҲ’1 be fixed during the training, and ОІj = Оҳ(1/n) > 0, j вҲҲ N

356   This theorem reflects the intuition that 1) selecting class-relevant tokens is desirable for generaliza-

358   data. The proof provided in Section C demonstrates such token selection is performed.
359
360   4.2   C ONVERGENCE TO B ENIGN OVERFITTING
361
362
      While we have seen the existence of a benign overfitting solution, we will further analyze in this
      section whether gradient descent converges to such a solution and what conditions are necessary
363
      for benign overfitting. Theorem 4.1 shows that both the signal required for generalization and the
364
      noise memorization for fitting label noise are significant, and their balance is essential for benign
365   overfitting. In practice, the model is trained by gradient descent based on the empirical loss function;
366   therefore, we must handle the influence of label noise in the signal learning, and noise memorization
367                                                               (j)
      occurs for all tokens besides the desirable token uj вҲҲ WY (j) , j вҲҲ N , making it challenging to draw
368   simple conclusions for optimization.
369
370   Before presenting the statement, we introduce the following value, which is crucial for understand-
371
      ing the gradient descent behavior in the attention architecture.
372   Definition 4.1 (Uncertainty in probability of picking relevant token). For any i вҲҲ [n] and time step
373   П„ вүҘ 0, we define S(i) (П„ ) вҲҲ [0, 1/4] as:
374                                                       !                   !
                                              X                    X
375                               (i)              (i)                  (i)
                                S (П„ ) :=         s (П„ )t      1вҲ’      s (П„ )t ,                       (7)
376                                            tвҲҲR                     tвҲҲR
377
      where recall that s(i) (П„ ) вҲҲ RT be a shorthand for the softmax probability S(X(i) WвҠӨ p(П„ )).


                                                             7
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


378
      This value can be interpreted as the variance of the Bernoulli distribution, where the parameter is
379   the attention probability assigned to the relevant token of training sample i вҲҲ [n] at time step П„ .
380   This behavior is crucial in the analysis of signal learning. Specifically, in learning signal vectors, the
381   balance between the contributions of S(П„ ) from clean data and from noisy data is significant and
382   determines the progress of signal learning at time step П„ . Figure 2 illustrates the training dynamics
383   of this quantity for clean and noisy data. As shown later, the softmax probability converges to either
384   0 or 1 for any token under the parameter assumptions. Therefore, S(П„ ) will eventually converge to
385   0 as illustrated in Figure 2.
386   By dividing cases for the behavior of S, we obtain the following results for the convergence.
387
      Theorem 4.2 (Convergence of benign overfitting solution). Suppose that the assumptions (AвҖҷ1)-
388   (AвҖҷ3), (A4)-(A6) hold, and the norm of the fixed linear head ОҪ вҲқ Вө+1 вҲ’ ВөвҲ’1 scales as Оҳ(1/вҲҘВөвҲҘ2 ).
389   With probability of at least 1 вҲ’ Оҙ, there exists a sufficiently large time step T0 , and for all time step
390   after it: П„ вүҘ T0 , the model overfits to the training data S with label noise:
391

392

423
      This theorem shows that under the assumptions in Section 3.5, the model overfits the training data
      with gradient descent, and it further reduces the discussion of generalization ability to the accumu-
424
      lation of S during the training. Eqs. 11 and 13 are regarded as conditions on the balance between
425
      the contributions of clean and noisy data. The reason for requiring such conditions on S will be
426   discussed later in the second paragraph of Section 4.3. The condition Eq.9 requires that the total
427   sum of the cumulative S over the clean data exceeds the accumulation of S for each data point.
428   The condition Eq.10 concerns the balance of the cumulative S among classes, and in the existing
429   analysis not involving softmax probability, it is straightforward to verify that the number of samples
430   for each class in the training data is equal ignoring constants with high probability. We will provide
431   the experiments that discuss the validity of Eqs.9 and 10 in Section I. The proof sketch for the main
      theorems is provided in Section A.2, while the proof of Theorem 4.2 is detailed in Section E.


                                                            8
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


432
      4.3      D IFFICULTY S PECIFIC TO ATTENTION A NALYSIS
433
434   In this section, we compare our work with the existing analysis of benign overfitting (Chatterji
435   & Long, 2021; Frei et al., 2022; Xu & Gu, 2023) tracking gradient descent dynamics, which is
436   the approach we took in this work, and highlight the unique difficulties inherent in the attention
437   architecture. The first two paragraphs focus on these difficulties, and in the final part, we explain the
438   limitations of the other approach.
439
440   Presence of local minima. The first difficulty comes from the presence of local minima that do
441
      not fit all the training data. In the previous settings, the gradient of the loss function (or the margin
      increase) at each time step can be bounded below by some form of training error; therefore, proving
442
      convergence of the gradient implies overfitting to the training set. In this paper setting, the gradient
443
      of the empirical loss function is given as follows:
444                                                         пЈ«          пЈ«                      пЈ¶         пЈ¶
                          n
445                    1 X                                    X    (i)   (i)
                                                                                X                   (i)
446     вҲҮp L(p)
            b      =         в„“вҖІ Y (i) В· f (X(i) ) В· Y (i) В· пЈӯ    st пЈӯОіt вҲ’            s(i)
                                                                                      u Оіu
                                                                                          (i) пЈё
                                                                                                Wxt пЈё , (15)
                       n i=1
447                                                        tвҲҲ[T ]             uвҲҲ[T ]
                вҖІ
448   where в„“ is the derivative of the binary cross-entropy loss, given in Eq.3. Since the learning token
449   selection does not change the scale of output, the в„“вҖІ term does not converge to zero, and the gra-
450   dient becomes zero only when the attention probabilities converge to 1 or 0, as will be shown in
451   Lemma D.3. Therefore, undesirable tokens can be picked even when the gradient becomes zero,
452   and we have to track the dynamics of token probability during training. In the appendix, we not only
453
      analyzed the convergence of the training loss gradient but also tracked the probabilities assigned to
      each token, identifying which token converges to 1 and is thus selected by the model.
454
455
      Diminishing parameter updates due to softmax. The second difficulty is that the gradient de-
456
      scent updates are involved with the softmax probabilities. The relationship between the contribu-
457   tions from clean and noisy data, as well as the updates for signals and noises, cannot be evalu-
458   ated independently of the current time step, as the gradient descent updates depend on the value of
459   st (П„ )(1 вҲ’ st (П„ )). As shown in Figure 2, the closer the probability of selecting the desired token
460   approaches 1, the smaller the updates will be for selecting that token. This diminishing growth of
461   model parameters complicates the convergence analysis of benign overfitting in the attention, com-
462   pared to existing work on linear classifiers and two-layer networks. For example, while relevant
463   tokens in the clean data are quickly picked, driving S(П„ ) toward 0, if S(П„ ) is still large in noisy data
464   (i.e., not yet near both ends in Figure 2), the contribution of noisy data to the learning can become
465
      more significant than that of clean data even when the number of noisy data is much smaller. To
      address this, we introduced a metric in Definition 4.1, which allows us to present scenarios that lead
466
      to either benign or harmful overfitting, as demonstrated in Eqs. 11 and 13.
467
468
      Obstacles in approach from max-margin problem One potential approach is based on the con-
469
      vergence of max-margin solutions for token-separation (Tarzanagh et al., 2023b; Vasudeva et al.,
470   2024), similar to the existing work using KKT conditions. However, this approach is challenging
471   for two reasons: 1) there is no guarantee of global convergence of the max-margin problem, and 2)
472   there is a mismatch between the max-margin solution and the local solution of empirical loss due to
473   the difference in token scores. Please refer to Section H.3 in the appendix for further details.
474
475
      5       E XPERIMENTS
476
477   In this section, we conduct synthetic experiments based on the data model in Definition 3.1 to
478   support our analysis. The code used for the experiments is available on GitHub 1 . For more detailed
479   experiments varying d and вҲҘВөвҲҘ2 , as well as experiments on real-world image and text datasets
480   containing label noise, please refer to Section I in the appendix.
481
      We train the same model f as in Eq.2 with gradient descent, using the same data model as Defi-
482
      nition 3.1 with the covariance matrix ОЈ = I. Specifically, we consider the setting with n = 20,
483   T = 8, О· = 0.2, ПҒ = 0.2 and Оұ =1eвҲ’4, changing the value of the dimension d = Tr(ОЈ) and the
484   signal size вҲҘВөвҲҘ2 . For simplicity, we set |R| = 1 and |W+1 | = |WвҲ’1 | = 1.

509   Figure 3: Dynamics of softmax probability. The top represents a clean sample, while the bottom
510   represents a noisy sample for each column. From left to right, the figures correspond to the cases of
511   benign overfitting, harmful overfitting, and not overfitting.
512
513
514   Figure 3 shows the dynamics of softmax probabilities for clean and noisy training samples from
515   the initial value p(0) = 0. The left figure shows the case where the balance between signal and
516   noise satisfies the theorem assumptions (see assumptions (A1) and (A2)), and benign overfitting is
517
      achieved. In this figure, selecting the weakly relevant token u вҲҲ WвҲ’Y вҲ—(j) for the noisy data j вҲҲ N
      aligns with our analysis. In the middle, where the d is larger compared to вҲҘВөвҲҘ2 , memorization
518
      becomes dominant. The bottom figure shows that weakly relevant tokens are not always selected for
519
      noisy data; instead, irrelevant tokens that fit the label noise are picked. This model can fit the training
520   data with noise components, which hinders signal learning and reduces generalization ability. In the
521   right figure, where signal norm вҲҘВөвҲҘ2 is large, fitting the noisy data becomes challenging. The middle
522   of Figure 3c shows that the model successfully selects weakly relevant tokens aligning with the label
523   noise, but in the bottom figure, some noisy data are influenced toward selecting relevant tokens that
524   should not be chosen for fitting noisy data. We can see that the balance between the dimension and
525   the signal norm, as well as a low label noise, is significant for achieving low train and test loss.
526
527
      6    C ONCLUSION
528
529
      In this paper, we analyzed benign overfitting in the token selection of the attention architecture and
530   further supported the analysis with synthetic experiments. As a natural next step, a more detailed
531   technical analysis of the attention probability behavior during training, which appeared as condi-
532   tions in our results, can be considered. Additionally, extending the attention analysis to next-token
533   prediction or a full self-attention model is also an interesting research direction.

953   A.2     P ROOF S KETCH
954
955   In this section, we briefly introduce the proof sketch of the main theorem along with the key lemmas
956   to provide the road maps in the appendix. Table 2 provides a concise comparison of analysis ap-
957   proaches with the existing benign overfitting work for classification. We hope this will help further
958   understanding through comparison with other papers.
959   The whole proof of Theorem 4.1 is provided in Section C, and the proof of Theorem 4.2 is completed
960   in Section E. We first show that the following events occur simultaneously with high probability
961   under the assumptions in Section 3.5.

       Definition A.1. We denote by вҖңgood runвҖқ the trial that the events from (i) to (iv) occur.
982
983    In the proof of the main theorems, we discuss the properties that occur with high probability by
984    conditioning on these events. Lemma A.1 implies that a good run occurs with the probability at least
985    1 вҲ’ Оҙ over the realization of training data S. The proof of Lemma A.1 will be provided in Section B.
986    The proof of Theorem 4.1 is based on these concentration inequalities and detailed evaluations of
987    the softmax probabilities. For the complete proof, please refer to Section C.
988    The convergence analysis, due to the difficulties stated in Section 4.3, follows an approach of track-
989    ing the dynamics of the signal and noise components in the gradient method rather than setting
990    desired converged weights and evaluating the alignment with them. Specifically, let p(П„ ) be a gra-
991    dient iteration at П„ -th time step; then, there exists unique coefficients such that

1040   The following lemma indicates that the dynamics of W can be described by the dynamics of p.
1041   It justifies optimizing p alone while keeping W fixed throughout training. This is also intuitively
1042   understood from the fact that the gradient update of W always results in a rank-1 matrix. Since
1043   some modifications have been made to the original form, we will provide the statement with proof.

1911
       Remark 4 (Ratio of Loss Derivative). This lemma, which shows that the gradients of loss function
1912   for clean data and noisy data remain within a constant factor of each other at every time step, is
1913   a critical component of the proof in the existing analyses of linear classifiers and two-layer neural
1914   networks (Chatterji & Long, 2021; Frei et al., 2022; Xu & Gu, 2023). However, in the learning
1915   of token selection, the output is always an affine combination of the token scores, and the output
1916   scale is not changed. Therefore, as long as the balance of the loss derivatives in the token scores is
1917   maintained, the training process itself need not be considered. To ensure that the derivative of the
1918   loss function for each token remains within a constant factor, a small linear head scale, as described
1919   in Lemma D.2, is required. If the scale of the linear head is too large, little gradient will be generated
1920
       for clean data even at the initial weights, and learning the signal vectors will not progress.
1921   Lemma D.3. Suppose that the gradient of loss function satisfies limП„ вҶ’вҲһ вҲҘвҲҮp Lb (p(П„ )) вҲҘ2 = 0, and
1922   the assumption (A4) for the step size Оұ holds. Then there exists the token index tвҲ—i вҲҲ [T ] for each
1923   i вҲҲ [n], and we have
1924                                          (i)                                    (i)
                                         lim s вҲ— (П„ ) = 1,          and      lim st (П„ ) = 0,                 (150)
1925                                    П„ вҶ’вҲһ ti                           П„ вҶ’вҲһ
1926
       for all i вҲҲ [n], t вҲҲ [T ] \ {tвҲ—i }.
1927
1928
1929
       Proof. We first show the technical result that for linearly independent vectors v1 , . . . , vm вҲҲ Rd and
       coefficients a1 , . . . , am вҲҲ R, there exists a constant c > 0 such that
1930
1931                                                X                        X
1932
                                                           ai v i       вүҘc           |ai |.                   (151)
                                                   iвҲҲ[m]            2        iвҲҲ[m]
1933
                                     P
1934   We can discuss the case for iвҲҲ[m] |ai | = 1 without loss of generality. Considering the map from
1935   the unit sphere of в„“m
                                                              P
                           1 to R via (a1 , . . . , am ) 7вҶ’ вҲҘ  iвҲҲ[m] ai vi вҲҘ2 , since this map is continuous and the
1936   domain it compact, this function attains a minimum value. The linear independence of v1 , . . . , vm
1937   implies this minimum value is positive, and we take this value as c > 0 and thus conclude Eq.151.

2083   Remark 5. We explain the reason for considering the worst-case scenario where the signal update
2084   вҲҶО»(П„ ) becomes negative, as in Eq. 162. In the initial training phase, the signal learning progresses
2085   in a positive direction because of the noise ratio assumption, assigning more probability to the rel-
2086   evant token of the clean data. If we can show that for all clean data i вҲҲ C, maxtвҲҲR s(i) (П„ )t
2087   becomes a constant order after this initial phase, for example, greater than 1/2 instead of around
2088
       the initial value Оҳ(1/T ), it would be possible to prove Lemma A.3 under the weaker strength of
       memorization, Tr(ОЈ) вүҘ CT nвҲҘВөвҲҘ22 .PHowever, considering the case where most clean data pick
2089
       the relevant token very quickly (i.e., tвҲҲR s(i) (П„ )t вү« 1 вҲ’ 1/T ), while some clean data is learned
2090                           вҖІ
       slowly (i.e., tвҲҲR s(i ) (П„ )t = Оҳ(|R|/T )), the signal update might be a negative direction because
                     P
2091
2092
       of the discussion in Section 4.3. This leads to a situation where the relevant token will not be picked
       for these slowly learned clean data. Therefore, we proceed with the inequality Tr(ОЈ) вүҘ CT 2 nвҲҘВөвҲҘ22
2093
       to ensure that the relevant token is selected for all clean data regardless of the positive or negative of
2094
       the signal update.

2781   In this section, we will see that the analysis in the multi-class setting is a straightforward extension

       the multi-class setting.

2912   H.1   F URTHER D ETAILS ON CONSTANT SIZE ASSUMPTION OF R AND W
2913
2914   In this section, we show that we can remove the assumptions introduced in our data model defined
2915   in Section 3.3, that the numbers of relevant tokens and weakly relevant tokens are constant across
       all examples. This assumption was introduced in the main text for the sake of notational simplicity.


                                                        54
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


2916
       To relax this simplification, we denote the index set of relevant, weakly relevant, and irrelevant
2917
       tokens for the input X(i) by R(i) вҲҲ [T ], W (i) вҲҲ [T ] \ R(i) , and I (i) = [T ] \ (R(i) вҲӘ W (i) ),
2918
       respectively. We still assume that each token set contains at least one token as in the original setting.
2919   We then provide comments on the necessary adjustments to the arguments of the paper.
2920
2921   First, no modifications are required for the proof of Theorem 4.1. In the part of demonstrating
2922
       overfitting, the claim remains valid because at least one relevant token exists for each example.
       Regarding the discussion on convergence, the metric defined in Definition 4.1, which is related to
2923
       the softmax probability of relevant tokens, is updated as follows:
2924
                                           пЈ«                 пЈ¶пЈ«                      пЈ¶
2925                                         X                        X
2926                          S(i) (П„ ) := пЈӯ      s(i) (П„ )t пЈё пЈӯ1 вҲ’       s(i) (П„ )t пЈё ,            (276)
2927                                         tвҲҲR(i)                  tвҲҲR(i)
2928
2929   which is slightly modified from the original definition using R(i) instead of R. We have to update
2930   this part; however, no substantial changes other than notations are required for the proof of The-
2931   orem 4.2. This is because the convergence discussion relies on the conditions 1 вүӨ |R| вүӨ T вҲ’ 1
2932
       (similarly for W and I), and the discussion is based on the inequalities up to constant factors for
       the worst-case scenario. The assumption that R, W, and I are common across examples is entirely
2933
       unnecessary for such asymptotic evaluations.

       H.2   FAILURE OF T RAINING L INEAR C LASSIFIERS WITH U NIFORM ATTENTION
2937
       In this section, we demonstrate that training a linear head ОҪ on top of uniform attention (p = 0)
2938   does not work well within our data model, from the perspective of benign overfitting. This discussion
2939   supports our data model setting because it is a sufficiently challenging setting so that a fixed linear
2940   head ОҪ alone is inadequate.
2941
       We present two scenarios to explain this situation. Since we consider   the setting of uniform attention,
2942
       the model is given by f (X; ОҪ) = ОҪ вҠӨ XвҠӨ 1/T . Let xМ„ :=
                                                                      P
2943                                                                     tвҲҲ[T ] xi /T be the average of input

2944   sequence X, where X is defined in Definition 3.1. We can write f (X; ОҪ) = ОҪ вҠӨ xМ„ with this notation.
2945
       For simplicity, in the discussion of this section, we assume that the input tokens have zero means
       and treat Вө+1 вҲ’ ВөвҲ’1 , вҲ’Вө+1 + ВөвҲ’1 as the class signals for Y = +1, вҲ’1, respectively. Please note
2946
       that this does not change the signal strength ignoring constants.

2948

2949
       where every input X has one relevant token, and all other tokens are irrelevant tokens. The following

2951   the generalization error of the linear max-margin classifier.

2990
2991
       Scenario 2: One Relevant Token and Confusing Weakly Relevant Tokens. This scenario is a
2992
       more extreme case compared to the first scenario. We consider the setting where the inputs in the
2993
       training data X(i) have one relevant token, and all other tokens are the weakly relevant tokens that
2994
       have the confusing class signals, i.e., belong to WвҲ’Y вҲ—(i) . When the scale of the weakly relevant
2995
       token is constant, i.e. ПҒ = Оҳ(1) < 1, then the averaged input xМ„(i) could align with confusing
2996
       class signal ВөвҲ’Y вҲ—(i) more than the true class signals ВөY вҲ—(i) , depending on the sequence length T .
2997
       The linear classifier ОҪ trained on such data fails completely to classify unseen samples X. In this
2998   case, classification of the input sequence through token averaging fails, and selecting the token that
2999   contains the most class-relevant token (relevant token) becomes essentially significant.
3000
3001   H.3    A DDITIONAL I NFORMATION FOR S ECTION 4.3
3002
3003   We provide the supplementary notes for Section 4.3, specifically focusing on the two reasons men-
3004   tioned for the difficulties in the approach from the max-margin problem.
3005   The first reason is that the optimization of the empirical loss function is a non-convex optimiza-
3006   tion due to the softmax function, which does not guarantee global convergence. Without strong
3007   assumptions on the initial values or the nature of the data, there is no guarantee that the weights will
3008   converge to a max-margin solution (Tarzanagh et al., 2023b; Vasudeva et al., 2024).
3009
       The second reason is concerned with the formulation of the following token separation problem:
3010
                                                                (i)    (i)
3011                 arg min вҲҘpвҲҘ2 subject to minвҲ— pвҠӨ W xtвҲ— вҲ’ xt              вүҘ 1, вҲҖi вҲҲ [n],
                        pвҲҲRd                        tМё=ti           i
3012
3013   where tвҲ—i вҲҲ [T ] is the token index to pick up for the input X(i) . For the local solution of the
3014   empirical loss function to match the max-margin solution of this problem, simplifying assumptions
3015   are necessary, such as identical token scores Оі of unpicked tokens. In our analysis, the noise in the
3016   token scores is significant for fitting the noisy data, so the difference in token scores among noise
3017   vectors cannot be ignored. In addition, we consider a more general setting where there are multiple
3018   relevant tokens, which have large token scores.

3094
3095   I.2   R EAL -W ORLD E XPERIMENTS
3096
3097   We further conducted real-world experiments on image and natural language datasets for classifica-
3098
       tion. For each task, we prepared the pre-trained ViT (Dosovitskiy et al., 2021) and BERT (Devlin
       et al., 2018) models using huggingface transformer library (Wolf et al., 2020). These models use the
3099
       default configuration; specifically, they consist of 12 attention layers, the embedding dimension is
3100
       set to 768, and the hidden dimension of the feed-forward layers is set to 3072. Since this is an exper-
3101   iment on overfitted models, dropout was not performed in any layers during the following training.
3102   We fine-tuned the models to the downstream tasks in the following two steps.
3103
3104   Step 1: Train only the classifier head on a subset of the training data without label noise.
3105
3106   Step 2: Train only the last attention weights on another subset of the training data with label noise.
3107
3108   In the first step, the classifier head is trained to adapt to the downstream task from the random initial
3109
       values in the pre-trained model. At this time, only the last classifier is trained and label noise is not
       included. This step reflects our analysis setting in Section 3 that the linear head ОҪ is fixed in the
3110
       class signal direction Вө.
3111
3112   In the second step, only the last attention query-key weights are trained while keeping the classifier
3113   head fixed. We trained models on a sub-dataset, including label noise, that does not overlap with that
3114   in the first step until sufficient overfitting is achieved. This step can be corresponded to the settings
3115
       of our theoretical analysis in Section 3 as follows. If the output of the backbone model up to the
       last attention layer is considered a fixed feature extractor, then the features of each data become the
3116
       input for the final attention layer, which is the trainable one-layer model. Since the output of the
3117
       CLS token position is passed to the classifier head, this setting corresponds to the scenario where
3118   the attention weight W is trained for a CLS tokenвҖҷs feature p. We confirmed in Lemma A.6 that the
3119   same results follow when optimizing p for a fixed W, which is the setting discussed in this paper.
3120   However, strictly speaking, please note that there is a difference from the theoretical setting in that
3121   the feature of the CLS token is not fixed for each data due to the use of an attention model as a
3122   feature extractor.
3123
       The used datasets are described as follows. Since this experiment focuses on analyzing the general-
3124   ization ability under overfitting, the sizes of the sub-datasets in Step 1 and Step 2 are set to small,
3125   5000 and 500, respectively. For datasets with small sizes of training datasets, the smaller sizes are
3126   used and explicitly stated in Tables 3 and 4.
3127
3128   Image Dataset We conducted experiments on image classification on the following five datasets.
3129   The MNIST (LeCun et al., 2010) dataset consists of gray-scale 28Г—28 images with 10 classes. Each
3130   image is copied to form a 3-channel input and fed to the common image processor for the pre-trained
3131   ViT model. The CIFAR-10 (Krizhevsky, 2009) is the dataset composed of 32 Г— 32 color images in
       10 classes. These classes are mainly made up of vehicles and animals. The STL-10 dataset (Coates

3156   et al., 2011) is also a 10-class classification dataset inspired by the CIFAR-10, but each image has a
3157   size of 96Г—96 pixels. Finally, we focused on the MedMNIST (Yang et al., 2023) dataset, specifically
3158   using PneumoniaMNIST and BreastMNIST, which are tasks for disease detection. Both datasets
3159   consist of 28 Г— 28 gray-scale images similar to MNIST, and they are binary classification settings
3160   based on the presence or absence of disease. Table 3 summarizes the details of these datasets, as
3161   well as the sizes of the sub-datasets used in Step 1 and Step 2.
3162   Furthermore, we provide comments on how our data model, based on relevant, weakly relevant, and
3163   irrelevant tokens defined in Definition 3.1, corresponds to the input images. For example, consider
3164   the case of cancer detection using medical images such as MedMNIST. In this case, the patches
3165   directly indicating cancer, such as tumors or lesions, can considered relevant tokens. The patches
3166   showing enlarged lymph nodes or inflammatory signs, which often co-occur with cancer but are
3167   not definite, can be categorized as weakly relevant tokens. Meanwhile, the patches showing normal
3168   tissue or background anatomy, which are irrelevant to the task, correspond to irrelevant tokens.

3171
       Natural Language Dataset We conducted experiments on sentence classification in natural lan-
       guage in addition to image data. The SST-2 (Socher et al., 2013) from GLUE benchmark (Wang
3172
       et al., 2019) is the dataset for the sentiment analysis, i.e., binary classification with positive and neg-
3173
       ative. The AG-news (Zhang et al., 2015) is the dataset for the topic classification of news articles.
3174   It has four largest classes: вҖңworldвҖқ, вҖңsportsвҖқ, вҖңbusinessвҖқ, and вҖңscience/technologyвҖқ. The TREC (Li
3175   & Roth, 2002) dataset is the dataset for question classification in 6 classes. Each question is labeled
3176   based on the content and the question type. The details of these datasets are summarized in Table 4.
3177   Note that the text data can also correspond to the data model in the paper based on the relevance of
3178   each word to the class, similar to the case of images.
3179
       The experimental results are provided in Table 5. During the training, the AdamW optimizer
3180   (Loshchilov & Hutter, 2019) was used with a learning rate of 5eвҲ’5, along with linear warmup
3181   and learning rate decay. For all datasets, we trained the classification head for 250 epochs in Step
3182   1, and we trained only the last attention query-key weights for 200 epochs in Step 2. In Step 2,
3183   we increased 100 epochs for every 0.1 increase in label noise ratio because the convergence speed
3184   slows down with data containing label noise. Table 5 presents the test accuracy after Step 2, where
3185   the model is sufficiently trained on the noisy sub-datasets. The training loss is included in the table
       to see the degree of fit to the training set. Please note that in this setup, since only the query-key


                                                           59
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


3186
       weights are trained while value matrices and feed-forward layers are fixed, the scale of outputs does
3187   not change, and it is not possible to reduce the training loss to 0 depending on the dataset.
3188
3189   We first discuss the degree of overfitting observed in our experiments. For tasks such as MedMNIST
3190
       (pneumonia, breast) and text classification, the model shows relatively higher overfitting on the noisy
       dataset. In contrast, for datasets such as MNIST, CIFAR10, and STL-10, we can see that fitting data
3191
       with label noise is difficult when only the query-key weights of the final layer are trained. The
3192
       potential reasons for these differences are the following.
3193
3194         1. Dataset: As shown in Thoerem 4.1, fitting label noise requires the presence of tokens that
3195            can explain the label noise. This scenario becomes difficult with random label noise as in
3196            this experiment setup. The model may overfit more extensively with label noise arising
3197            from the presence of confusing tokens, such as annotation errors.
3198         2. Feature extractor: In this experiment, we treat the backbone model up to the last layer as a
3199            fixed feature extractor. By the same reason in the first point, if the feature extractor effec-
3200            tively suppresses the influence of noisy tokens, overfitting becomes inherently difficult.
3201         3. Parameter size: Overparameterization is essential to select noisy tokens over relevant to-
3202            kens, leading to overfitting. Achieving more overfitting requires increasing the parameter
3203            size from the attention dimension 768 in this experiment. Please also refer to our theoretical
3204            assumptions, such as assumption (AвҖҷ1), in the main text.
3205
3206   We also discuss the generalization ability in this result. Compared to the ideal setting where there
3207
       is no label noise, datasets such as PneumoniaMNIST, CIFAR10, STL-10, BreastMNIST, and SST-2
       exhibit minimal changes of generalization error in 0.1 noise ratio. On the other hand, datasets like
3208
       AG-news and TREC show more significant decreases in generalization ability. In the following, we
3209
       provide the potential factors contributing to these differences.
3210
3211         1. Presence of tokens fitting label noise: When no tokens exist to fit the label noise, the model
3212            not only fails to overfit but also avoids selecting the desirable tokens to reduce the training
3213            error. This worsens the generalization of the model.
3214         2. Signal strength: If the class signal is insufficiently captured, the noisy components learned
3215            to select noisy tokens to fit label noise may interfere with the selection of truly relevant
3216            tokens, leading to a decline in generalization ability. Please also see assumption (AвҖҷ2) in
3217            the main text.
3218         3. Parameter size: As mentioned in the overfitting part, further overparameterization increases
3219            the signal strength and noisy components relative to the noisy dataset size n, thereby bridg-
3220            ing the gap between the setting of this experiment and the theory in the main text.
3221
3222   Additionally, the table shows that the generalization error increases with the label noise. In our
3223   Theorem 4.2, the condition Eq. 11 addresses the balance of contributions between clean and noisy
3224
       samples, but the possible constant C3 becomes smaller as label noise increases, under the same
       setting of model and datasets.
